codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="513720" data-framerate="30" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="480" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/ba/A_day_with_Thomas_A._Edison.webm/A_day_with_Thomas_A._Edison.webm.360p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="519624" data-framerate="30" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="480" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/ba/A_day_with_Thomas_A._Edison.webm/A_day_with_Thomas_A._Edison.webm.360p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_day_with_Thomas_A._Edison.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>A Day with Thomas Edison</i> (1922)</div></div></div>
<p>As the film business expanded, competing exhibitors routinely copied and exhibited each other's films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> To better protect the copyrights on his films, Edison deposited prints of them on long strips of <a href="/wiki/Photographic_paper" title="Photographic paper">photographic paper</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._copyright_office" title="U.S. copyright office">U.S. copyright office</a>. Many of these paper prints survived longer and in better condition than the actual films of that era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1908, Edison started the <a href="/wiki/Motion_Picture_Patents_Company" title="Motion Picture Patents Company">Motion Picture Patents Company</a>, which was a conglomerate of nine major film studios (commonly known as the Edison Trust). Thomas Edison was the first honorary fellow of the <a href="/wiki/Acoustical_Society_of_America" title="Acoustical Society of America">Acoustical Society of America</a>, which was founded in 1929.
</p><p>Edison said his favorite movie was <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_a_Nation" title="The Birth of a Nation">The Birth of a Nation</a></i>. He thought that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talkies" title="Talkies">talkies</a> had "spoiled everything" for him. "There isn't any good acting on the screen. They concentrate on the voice now and have forgotten how to act. I can sense it more than you because I am deaf."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-condensed1042_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-condensed1042-101">[101]</a></sup> His favorite stars were <a href="/wiki/Mary_Pickford" title="Mary Pickford">Mary Pickford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clara_Bow" title="Clara Bow">Clara Bow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining">Mining</span></h3>
<p>Starting in the late 1870s, Edison became interested and involved with mining. High-grade iron ore was scarce on the east coast of the United States and Edison tried to mine low-grade ore. Edison developed a process using rollers and crushers that could pulverize rocks up to 10 tons. The dust was then sent between three giant magnets that would pull the iron ore from the dust. Despite the failure of his mining company, the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Ore-Milling_Company" title="Edison Ore-Milling Company">Edison Ore Milling Company</a>, Edison used some of the materials and equipment to produce cement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1901, Edison visited an industrial exhibition in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Sudbury" title="Greater Sudbury">Sudbury</a> area in Ontario, Canada and thought nickel and cobalt deposits there could be used in his production of electrical equipment. He returned as a mining prospector and is credited with the original discovery of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falconbridge,_Greater_Sudbury,_Ontario" title="Falconbridge, Greater Sudbury, Ontario">Falconbridge</a> ore body. His attempts to mine the ore body were not successful, and he abandoned his mining claim in 1903.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> A street in Falconbridge, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edison_Building_(Falconbridge)" title="Edison Building (Falconbridge)">Edison Building</a>, which served as the head office of <a href="/wiki/Falconbridge_Ltd." title="Falconbridge Ltd.">Falconbridge Mines</a>, are named for him.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rechargeable_battery">Rechargeable battery</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93iron_battery#History" title="Nickel–iron battery">Nickel–iron battery § History</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edison_Storage_Battery_Company_1903.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2242" data-file-width="3101" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Edison_Storage_Battery_Company_1903.JPG/220px-Edison_Storage_Battery_Company_1903.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Edison_Storage_Battery_Company_1903.JPG/330px-Edison_Storage_Battery_Company_1903.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Edison_Storage_Battery_Company_1903.JPG/440px-Edison_Storage_Battery_Company_1903.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edison_Storage_Battery_Company_1903.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Share of the Edison Storage Battery Company, issued October 19, 1903</div></div></div>
<p>In the late 1890s, Edison worked on developing a lighter, more efficient <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">rechargeable battery</a> (at that time called an "accumulator"). He looked on them as something customers could use to power their phonographs but saw other uses for an improved battery, including <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric automobiles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The then available <a href="/wiki/Lead%E2%80%93acid_battery" title="Lead–acid battery">lead acid rechargeable batteries</a> were not very efficient and that market was already tied up by other companies so Edison pursued using <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkaline</a> instead of acid. He had his lab work on many types of materials (going through some 10,000 combinations), eventually settling on a nickel-iron combination. Besides his experimenting Edison also probably had access to the 1899 patents for a <a href="/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93iron_battery" title="Nickel–iron battery">nickel–iron battery</a> by the Swedish inventor <a href="/wiki/Waldemar_Jungner" title="Waldemar Jungner">Waldemar Jungner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seth_Fletcher_2011,_pages_14-16_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seth_Fletcher_2011,_pages_14-16-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Edison obtained a US and European patent for his nickel–iron battery in 1901 and founded the Edison Storage Battery Company, and by 1904 it had 450 people working there. The first rechargeable batteries they produced were for electric cars, but there were many defects, with customers complaining about the product. When the capital of the company was exhausted, Edison paid for the company with his private money. Edison did not demonstrate a mature product until 1910: a very efficient and durable nickel-iron-battery with lye as the electrolyte. The nickel–iron battery was never very successful; by the time it was ready, electric cars were disappearing, and lead acid batteries had become the standard for turning over gas-powered car <a href="/wiki/Starter_(engine)" title="Starter (engine)">starter motors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seth_Fletcher_2011,_pages_14-16_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seth_Fletcher_2011,_pages_14-16-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemicals">Chemicals</span></h3>
<p>At the start of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the American chemical industry was primitive.  Most chemicals were imported from Europe.  The outbreak of war in August 1914 resulted in an immediate shortage of imported chemicals.  One of particular importance to Edison was <a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">phenol</a>, which was used to make <a href="/wiki/Phonograph" title="Phonograph">phonograph</a> records—presumably as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenolic_resins" title="Phenolic resins">phenolic resins</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bakelite" title="Bakelite">Bakelite</a> type.
</p><p>At the time, phenol came from coal as a by-product of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coke_oven" title="Coke oven">coke oven</a> gases or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manufactured_gas" title="Manufactured gas">manufactured gas</a> for <a href="/wiki/Gas_lighting" title="Gas lighting">gas lighting</a>.  Phenol could be nitrated to <a href="/wiki/Picric_acid" title="Picric acid">picric acid</a> and converted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonium_picrate" title="Ammonium picrate">ammonium picrate</a>, a shock resistant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_explosive" title="High explosive">high explosive</a> suitable for use in artillery shells.  A telling of the phenol story is found in <i>The Aspirin Wars</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Most phenol had been imported from Britain, but with war, Parliament blocked exports and diverted most to production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonium_picrate" title="Ammonium picrate">ammonium picrate</a>.  Britain also blockaded supplies from Germany.
</p><p>Edison responded by undertaking production of phenol at his Silver Lake facility using processes developed by his chemists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> He built two plants with a capacity of six tons of phenol per day.  Production began the first week of September, one month after hostilities began in Europe.  He built two plants to produce raw material <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> at <a href="/wiki/Johnstown,_Pennsylvania" title="Johnstown, Pennsylvania">Johnstown, Pennsylvania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bessemer,_Alabama" title="Bessemer, Alabama">Bessemer, Alabama</a>, replacing supplies previously from Germany.  Edison also manufactured <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aniline_dyes" title="Aniline dyes">aniline dyes</a>, which previously had been supplied by the German dye trust.  Other wartime products include <a href="/wiki/Xylene" title="Xylene">xylene</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-phenylenediamine" title="P-phenylenediamine">p-phenylenediamine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shellac" title="Shellac">shellac</a>, and pyrax.  Wartime shortages made these ventures profitable.  In 1915, his production capacity was fully committed by midyear.
</p><p>Phenol was a critical material because two derivatives were in high growth phases. <a href="/wiki/Bakelite" title="Bakelite">Bakelite</a>, the original <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermoset" title="Thermoset">thermoset</a> plastic, had been invented in 1909. <a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">Aspirin</a>, too was a phenol derivative.  Invented in 1899, it had become a block buster drug. <a href="/wiki/Bayer" title="Bayer">Bayer</a> had acquired a plant to manufacture in the US in <a href="/wiki/Rensselaer,_New_York" title="Rensselaer, New York">Rensselaer, New York</a>, but struggled to find phenol to keep their plant running during the war.  Edison was able to oblige.
</p><p>Bayer relied on Chemische Fabrik von Heyden, in <a href="/wiki/Piscataway,_New_Jersey" title="Piscataway, New Jersey">Piscataway, New Jersey</a>, to convert phenol to salicylic acid, which they converted to aspirin.  (See Great Phenol plot.) It is said that German companies bought up supplies of phenol to block production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonium_picrate" title="Ammonium picrate">ammonium picrate</a>.  Edison preferred not to sell phenol for military uses.  He sold his surplus to Bayer, who had it converted to <a href="/wiki/Salicylic_acid" title="Salicylic acid">salicylic acid</a> by Heyden, some of which was exported.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spirit_Phone">Spirit Phone</span></h3>
<p>In 1920, Edison spoke to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Magazine" title="American Magazine">American Magazine</a>, saying that he had been working on a device for some time to see if it was possible to communicate with the dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlas_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atlas-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-forbes-110">[110]</a></sup> Edison said the device would work on scientific principles not by an occult means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlas_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Atlas-109">[109]</a></sup> The announcement caused a press heyday,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-forbes-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlas_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-Atlas-109">[109]</a></sup> though the actual nature of this invention remained a mystery, as there were no details for some time. Until In 2015, Philippe Baudouin, a French journalist, found a copy of Edison's Diary in a thrift store with a chapter not found in the previously published editions. The new chapter details Edison's theories of the afterlife and the scientific basis by which communication  with them might be achieved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlas_109-3"><a href="#cite_note-Atlas-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_and_death">Final years and death</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years">Final years</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="862" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg/220px-Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg/330px-Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg/440px-Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a>, Thomas Edison, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvey_Firestone" title="Harvey Firestone">Harvey Firestone</a>, respectively. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ft._Myers" title="Ft. Myers">Ft. Myers</a>, Florida, February 11, 1929</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a>, the automobile magnate, later lived a few hundred feet away from Edison at his winter retreat in Fort Myers. Ford once worked as an engineer for the <a href="/wiki/DTE_Electric_Company" title="DTE Electric Company">Edison Illuminating Company of Detroit</a> and met Edison at a convention of affiliated Edison illuminating companies in Brooklyn, NY in 1896. Edison was impressed with Ford's internal combustion engine automobile and encouraged its developments. They were friends until Edison's death. Edison and Ford undertook annual motor camping trips from 1914 to 1924. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvey_Firestone" title="Harvey Firestone">Harvey Firestone</a> and naturalist <a href="/wiki/John_Burroughs" title="John Burroughs">John Burroughs</a> also participated.
</p><p>In 1928, Edison joined the Fort Myers <a href="/wiki/Civitan_International" title="Civitan International">Civitan Club</a>. He believed strongly in the organization, writing that "The Civitan Club is doing things—big things—for the community, state, and nation, and I certainly consider it an honor to be numbered in its ranks."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> He was an active member in the club until his death, sometimes bringing Henry Ford to the club's meetings.
</p><p>Edison was active in business right up to the end. Just months before his death, the <a href="/wiki/Delaware,_Lackawanna_and_Western_Railroad" title="Delaware, Lackawanna and Western Railroad">Lackawanna Railroad</a> inaugurated suburban electric train service from <a href="/wiki/Hoboken,_New_Jersey" title="Hoboken, New Jersey">Hoboken</a> to <a href="/wiki/Montclair,_New_Jersey" title="Montclair, New Jersey">Montclair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dover,_New_Jersey" title="Dover, New Jersey">Dover</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gladstone,_New_Jersey" title="Gladstone, New Jersey">Gladstone, New Jersey</a>. Electrical transmission for this service was by means of an overhead catenary system using direct current, which Edison had championed. Despite his frail condition, Edison was at the throttle of the first electric MU (Multiple-Unit) train to depart Lackawanna Terminal in Hoboken in September 1930, driving the train the first mile through Hoboken yard on its way to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Orange,_NJ" title="South Orange, NJ">South Orange</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland_2001_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holland_2001-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>This fleet of cars would serve commuters in northern New Jersey for the next 54 years until their retirement in 1984. A plaque commemorating Edison's inaugural ride can be seen today in the waiting room of Lackawanna Terminal in Hoboken, which is presently operated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Transit" title="New Jersey Transit">New Jersey Transit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland_2001_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holland_2001-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Edison was said to have been influenced by a popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_faddism" title="Food faddism">fad diet</a> in his last few years; "the only liquid he consumed was a pint of milk every three hours".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Israel-54">[54]</a></sup> He is reported to have believed this diet would restore his health. However, this tale is doubtful. In 1930, the year before Edison died, Mina said in an interview about him, "Correct eating is one of his greatest hobbies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> She also said that during one of his periodic "great scientific adventures", Edison would be up at 7:00, have breakfast at 8:00, and be rarely home for lunch or dinner, implying that he continued to have all three.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-condensed1042_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-condensed1042-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Edison became the owner of his Milan, Ohio, birthplace in 1906. On his last visit, in 1923, he was reportedly shocked to find his old home still lit by lamps and candles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<p>Edison died of complications of diabetes on October 18, 1931, in his home, "Glenmont" in <a href="/wiki/Llewellyn_Park" title="Llewellyn Park">Llewellyn Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Orange,_New_Jersey" title="West Orange, New Jersey">West Orange, New Jersey</a>, which he had purchased in 1886 as a wedding gift for Mina. Rev. <a href="/wiki/Stephen_J._Herben" title="Stephen J. Herben">Stephen J. Herben</a> officiated at the funeral;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Edison is buried behind the home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Edison's last breath is reportedly contained in a test tube at <a href="/wiki/The_Henry_Ford" title="The Henry Ford">The Henry Ford</a> museum near Detroit. Ford reportedly convinced <a href="/wiki/Charles_Edison" title="Charles Edison">Charles Edison</a> to seal a test tube of air in the inventor's room shortly after his death, as a memento.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> A plaster <a href="/wiki/Death_mask" title="Death mask">death mask</a> and casts of Edison's hands were also made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Mina died in 1947.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriages_and_children">Marriages and children</span></h2>
<p>On December 25, 1871, at the age of 24, Edison married 16-year-old Mary Stilwell (1855–1884), whom he had met two months earlier; she was an employee at one of his shops. They had three children:
</p>
<ul><li>Marion Estelle Edison (1873–1965), nicknamed "Dot"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></li>
<li>Thomas Alva Edison Jr. (1876–1935), nicknamed "Dash"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></li>
<li>William Leslie Edison (1878–1937) Inventor, graduate of the Sheffield Scientific School at Yale, 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Mary Edison died at age 29 on August 9, 1884, of unknown causes: possibly from a <a href="/wiki/Brain_tumor" title="Brain tumor">brain tumor</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morphine_overdose" title="Morphine overdose">morphine overdose</a>. Doctors frequently prescribed morphine to women in those years to treat a variety of causes, and researchers believe that her symptoms could have been from morphine poisoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rutgers_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rutgers-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Edison generally preferred spending time in the laboratory to being with his family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time1979_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-time1979-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mina_Edison_1906.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="610" data-file-width="420" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mina_Edison_1906.jpg/220px-Mina_Edison_1906.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mina_Edison_1906.jpg/330px-Mina_Edison_1906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/77/Mina_Edison_1906.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mina_Edison_1906.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mina Miller Edison in 1906</div></div></div>
<p>On February 24, 1886, at the age of 39, Edison married the 20-year-old Mina Miller (1865–1947) in <a href="/wiki/Akron,_Ohio" title="Akron, Ohio">Akron, Ohio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> She was the daughter of the inventor <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Miller_(philanthropist)" title="Lewis Miller (philanthropist)">Lewis Miller</a>, co-founder of the <a href="/wiki/Chautauqua_Institution" title="Chautauqua Institution">Chautauqua Institution</a>, and a benefactor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist">Methodist</a> charities. They also had three children together:
</p>
<ul><li>Madeleine Edison (1888–1979), who married <a href="/wiki/John_Eyre_Sloane" title="John Eyre Sloane">John Eyre Sloane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Charles_Edison" title="Charles Edison">Charles Edison</a> (1890–1969), <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_Jersey" title="Governor of New Jersey">Governor of New Jersey</a> (1941–1944), who took over his father's company and experimental laboratories upon his father's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Theodore_Miller_Edison" title="Theodore Miller Edison">Theodore Miller Edison</a> (1898–1992), (MIT Physics 1923), credited with more than 80 patents.</li></ul>
<p>Mina outlived Thomas Edison, dying on August 24, 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wanting to be an inventor, but not having much of an aptitude for it, Thomas Edison's son, Thomas Alva Edison Jr., became a problem for his father and his father's business. Starting in the 1890s, Thomas Jr. became involved in <a href="/wiki/Snake_oil" title="Snake oil">snake oil</a> products and shady and fraudulent enterprises producing products being sold to the public as "The Latest Edison Discovery". The situation became so bad that Thomas Sr. had to take his son to court to stop the practices, finally agreeing to pay Thomas Jr. an allowance of $35 (equivalent to $1,056 in 2021)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-32">[32]</a></sup> per week, in exchange for not using the Edison name; the son began using aliases, such as Burton Willard. Thomas Jr., experiencing alcoholism, depression and ill health, worked at several menial jobs, but by 1931 (towards the end of his life) he would obtain a role in the Edison company, thanks to the intervention of his half-brother Charles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Views">Views</span></h2>
<h3><span id="On_religion.2C_and_metaphysics"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="On_religion,_and_metaphysics">On religion, and metaphysics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:19101002_%22No_Immortality_of_the_Soul%22_Says_Thomas_A._Edison_-_The_New_York_Times.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4542" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="468" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/19101002_%22No_Immortality_of_the_Soul%22_Says_Thomas_A._Edison_-_The_New_York_Times.jpg/330px-19101002_%22No_Immortality_of_the_Soul%22_Says_Thomas_A._Edison_-_The_New_York_Times.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/19101002_%22No_Immortality_of_the_Soul%22_Says_Thomas_A._Edison_-_The_New_York_Times.jpg/495px-19101002_%22No_Immortality_of_the_Soul%22_Says_Thomas_A._Edison_-_The_New_York_Times.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/19101002_%22No_Immortality_of_the_Soul%22_Says_Thomas_A._Edison_-_The_New_York_Times.jpg/660px-19101002_%22No_Immortality_of_the_Soul%22_Says_Thomas_A._Edison_-_The_New_York_Times.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:19101002_%22No_Immortality_of_the_Soul%22_Says_Thomas_A._Edison_-_The_New_York_Times.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This 1910 <i>New York Times</i> Magazine feature states that "Nature, the supreme power, (Edison) recognizes and respects, but does not worship. Nature is not merciful and loving, but wholly merciless, indifferent." Edison is quoted as saying "I am not an individual—I am an aggregate of cells, as, for instance, New York City is an aggregate of individuals. Will New York City go to heaven?"</div></div></div>
<p>Historian Paul Israel has characterized Edison as a "<a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">freethinker</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-Israel-54">[54]</a></sup> Edison was heavily influenced by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Reason" title="The Age of Reason">The Age of Reason</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-Israel-54">[54]</a></sup> Edison defended Paine's "scientific <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a>", saying, "He has been called an <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheist</a>, but atheist he was not. Paine believed in a supreme intelligence, as representing the idea which other men often express by the name of deity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-Israel-54">[54]</a></sup> In 1878, Edison joined the <a href="/wiki/Theosophical_Society" title="Theosophical Society">Theosophical Society</a> in New Jersey,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> but according to its founder, <a href="/wiki/Helena_Blavatsky" title="Helena Blavatsky">H. P. Blavatsky</a>, he was not a very active member.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> In an October 2, 1910, interview in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times_Magazine" title="New York Times Magazine">New York Times Magazine</a></i>, Edison stated:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Nature is what we know. We do not know the gods of religions. And nature is not kind, or merciful, or loving. If God made me—the fabled God of the three qualities of which I spoke: mercy, kindness, love—He also made the fish I catch and eat. And where do His mercy, kindness, and love for that fish come in? No; nature made us—nature did it all—not the gods of the religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Edison was accused of being an atheist for those remarks, and although he did not allow himself to be drawn into the controversy publicly, he clarified himself in a private letter:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>You have misunderstood the whole article, because you jumped to the conclusion that it denies the existence of God. There is no such denial, what you call God I call Nature, the Supreme intelligence that rules matter. All the article states is that it is doubtful in my opinion if our intelligence or soul or whatever one may call it lives hereafter as an entity or disperses back again from whence it came, scattered amongst the cells of which we are made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_54-6"><a href="#cite_note-Israel-54">[54]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>He also stated, "I do not believe in the God of the theologians; but that there is a Supreme Intelligence I do not doubt."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> In 1920, Edison set off a media sensation when he told <a href="/wiki/B._C._Forbes" title="B. C. Forbes">B. C. Forbes</a> of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Magazine" title="American Magazine">American Magazine</a></i> that he was working on a "spirit phone" to allow communication with the dead, a story which other newspapers and magazines repeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Edison later disclaimed the idea, telling the <i>New York Times</i> in 1926 that "I really had nothing to tell him, but I hated to disappoint him so I thought up this story about communicating with spirits, but it was all a joke."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_politics">On politics</span></h3>
<p>Edison was a supporter of <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage_in_the_United_States" title="Women's suffrage in the United States">women's suffrage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-139">[139]</a></sup> He said in 1915, "Every woman in this country is going to have the vote."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-139">[139]</a></sup> Edison notably signed onto a statement supporting women's suffrage which was published to counter <a href="/wiki/Anti-suffragism" title="Anti-suffragism">anti-suffragist</a> literature spread by Senator <a href="/wiki/James_Edgar_Martine" title="James Edgar Martine">James Edgar Martine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nonviolence" title="Nonviolence">Nonviolence</a> was key to Edison's political and moral views, and when asked to serve as a naval consultant for <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, he specified he would work only on defensive weapons and later noted, "I am proud of the fact that I never invented weapons to kill." Edison's philosophy of nonviolence extended to animals as well, about which he stated: "Nonviolence leads to the highest ethics, which is the goal of all evolution. Until we stop harming all other living beings, we are still savages."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> He was a vegetarian<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> but not a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegan" title="Vegan">vegan</a> in actual practice, at least near the end of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_54-7"><a href="#cite_note-Israel-54">[54]</a></sup> Following a tour of Europe in 1911, Edison spoke negatively about "the belligerent <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a> that he had sensed in every country he visited".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>Edison was an advocate for monetary reform in the United States. He was ardently opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> and debt-based money. Famously, he was quoted in the <i>New York Times</i> as stating: "Gold is a relic of <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>, and interest is an invention of Satan."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-query.nytimes.com_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-query.nytimes.com-145">[145]</a></sup> In the same article, he expounded upon the absurdity of a monetary system in which the taxpayer of the United States, in need of a loan, can be compelled to pay in return perhaps double the principal, or even greater sums, due to interest. Edison argued that, if the government can produce debt-based money, it could equally as well produce money that was a credit to the taxpayer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-query.nytimes.com_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-query.nytimes.com-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1922, he published a proposal, entitled "A Proposed Amendment to the Federal Reserve Banking System".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> In it, he detailed an explanation of a commodity-backed currency, in which the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> would issue interest-free currency to farmers, based on the value of commodities they produced. During a publicity tour that he took with friend and fellow inventor, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a>, he spoke publicly about his desire for monetary reform. For insight, he corresponded with prominent academic and banking professionals. In the end, however, Edison's proposals failed to find support and were abandoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards">Awards</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4761" data-file-width="5778" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Edison by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Archibald_Anderson" title="Abraham Archibald Anderson">Abraham Archibald Anderson</a> (1890), <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery_(United_States)" title="National Portrait Gallery (United States)">National Portrait Gallery</a></div></div></div><p>The following is an incomplete list of awards given to Edison during his lifetime and posthumously:
</p><ul><li>In 1878, Edison was awarded an honorary PhD from <a href="/wiki/Union_College" title="Union College">Union College</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/President_of_France" title="President of France">President</a> of the <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">Third French Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jules_Gr%C3%A9vy" title="Jules Grévy">Jules Grévy</a>, on the recommendation of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_(France)" title="Minister of Foreign Affairs (France)">Minister of Foreign Affairs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jules_Barth%C3%A9lemy-Saint-Hilaire" title="Jules Barthélemy-Saint-Hilaire">Jules Barthélemy-Saint-Hilaire</a>, and with the presentations of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_of_Posts,_Telegraphs,_and_Telephones_(France)" title="Minister of Posts, Telegraphs, and Telephones (France)">Minister of Posts and Telegraphs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Adolphe_Cochery" title="Louis Adolphe Cochery">Louis Cochery</a>, designated Edison with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%C3%A9gion_d%27honneur#The_Order_and_other_countries" title="Légion d'honneur"><i>distinction</i></a> of an <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honour" title="Legion of Honour">Officer of the Legion of Honour</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%C3%A9gion_d%27honneur" title="Légion d'honneur">Légion d'honneur</a>) by decree on November 10, 1881;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Edison was also named a Chevalier in the Legion in 1879, and a Commander in 1889.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-BIO-151">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1887, Edison won the <a href="/wiki/Matteucci_Medal" title="Matteucci Medal">Matteucci Medal</a>. In 1890, he was elected a member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences">Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_City_Council" title="Philadelphia City Council">Philadelphia City Council</a> named Edison the recipient of the <a href="/wiki/John_Scott_Medal" title="John Scott Medal">John Scott Medal</a> in 1889.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-BIO-151">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1899, Edison was awarded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_Longstreth_Medal" title="Edward Longstreth Medal">Edward Longstreth Medal</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Franklin_Institute" title="The Franklin Institute">The Franklin Institute</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LongstrethMedal_Laureates_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-LongstrethMedal_Laureates-152">[152]</a></sup></li>
<li>He was named an Honorable Consulting Engineer at the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase_Exposition" title="Louisiana Purchase Exposition">Louisiana Purchase Exposition</a> <a href="/wiki/World%27s_fair" title="World's fair">World's fair</a> in 1904.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_151-2"><a href="#cite_note-BIO-151">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1908, Edison received the American Association of Engineering Societies <a href="/wiki/John_Fritz_Medal" title="John Fritz Medal">John Fritz Medal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_151-3"><a href="#cite_note-BIO-151">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1915, Edison was awarded <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Medal" title="Franklin Medal">Franklin Medal</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Franklin_Institute" title="The Franklin Institute">The Franklin Institute</a> for discoveries contributing to the foundation of industries and the well-being of the human race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1920, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> department awarded him the <a href="/wiki/Navy_Distinguished_Service_Medal" title="Navy Distinguished Service Medal">Navy Distinguished Service Medal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_151-4"><a href="#cite_note-BIO-151">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1923, the <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Electrical_Engineers" title="American Institute of Electrical Engineers">American Institute of Electrical Engineers</a> created the Edison Medal and he was its first recipient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_151-5"><a href="#cite_note-BIO-151">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1927, he was granted membership in the <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_151-6"><a href="#cite_note-BIO-151">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li>On May 29, 1928, Edison received the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">Congressional Gold Medal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_151-7"><a href="#cite_note-BIO-151">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1983, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>, pursuant to Senate Joint Resolution 140 (Public Law 97–198), designated February 11, Edison's birthday, as National <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inventor%27s_Day" title="Inventor's Day">Inventor's Day</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i> magazine (USA), in a special double issue in 1997, placed Edison first in the list of the "100 Most Important People in the Last 1000 Years", noting that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_bulb" title="Light bulb">light bulb</a> he promoted "lit up the world". In the 2005 television series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Greatest_American" title="The Greatest American">The Greatest American</a></i>, he was voted by viewers as the fifteenth greatest.</li>
<li>In 2008, Edison was inducted in the <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Hall_of_Fame" title="New Jersey Hall of Fame">New Jersey Hall of Fame</a>.</li>
<li>In 2010, Edison was honored with a <a href="/wiki/Technical_Grammy_Award" title="Technical Grammy Award">Technical Grammy Award</a>.</li>
<li>In 2011, Edison was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneur_Walk_of_Fame" title="Entrepreneur Walk of Fame">Entrepreneur Walk of Fame</a> and named a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Floridian" title="Great Floridian">Great Floridian</a> by the governor and cabinet of Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tributes">Tributes</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Places_and_people_named_for_Edison">Places and people named for Edison</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1391" data-file-width="1278" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg/220px-Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg/330px-Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg/440px-Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thomas Edison commemorative stamp, issued on the 100th anniversary of his birth in 1947</div></div></div>
<p>Several places have been named after Edison, most notably the town of <a href="/wiki/Edison,_New_Jersey" title="Edison, New Jersey">Edison, New Jersey</a>. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison_State_University" title="Thomas Edison State University">Thomas Edison State University</a>, nationally known for adult learners, is in <a href="/wiki/Trenton,_New_Jersey" title="Trenton, New Jersey">Trenton, New Jersey</a>. Two community colleges are named for him: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edison_State_College" title="Edison State College">Edison State College</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Florida_SouthWestern_State_College" title="Florida SouthWestern State College">Florida SouthWestern State College</a>) in Fort Myers, Florida, and
Edison Community College in <a href="/wiki/Piqua,_Ohio" title="Piqua, Ohio">Piqua, Ohio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> There are numerous high schools named after Edison (see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Edison_High_School_(disambiguation)" title="Edison High School (disambiguation)">Edison High School</a>) and other schools including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Edison_Middle_School" title="Thomas A. Edison Middle School">Thomas A. Edison Middle School</a>. Footballer <a href="/wiki/Pel%C3%A9" title="Pelé">Pelé</a>'s father originally named him Edison, as a tribute to the inventor of the light bulb, but the name was incorrectly listed on his birth certificate as "Edson".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>The small town of <a href="/wiki/Alva,_Florida" title="Alva, Florida">Alva</a> just east of Fort Myers took Edison's middle name.
</p><p>In 1883, the City Hotel in <a href="/wiki/Sunbury,_Pennsylvania" title="Sunbury, Pennsylvania">Sunbury, Pennsylvania</a> was the first building to be lit with Edison's three-wire system. The hotel was renamed The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hotel_Edison_(Sunbury,_Pennsylvania)" title="Hotel Edison (Sunbury, Pennsylvania)">Hotel Edison</a> upon Edison's return to the city on 1922.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sunbury_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-sunbury-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1954, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Thomas_A_Edison" title="Lake Thomas A Edison">Lake Thomas A Edison</a> in California was named after Edison to mark the 75th anniversary of the <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">incandescent light bulb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sce_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-sce-159">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p>Edison was on hand to turn on the lights at the <a href="/wiki/Hotel_Edison" title="Hotel Edison">Hotel Edison</a> in New York City when it opened in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hotel_Edison_FAQ_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hotel_Edison_FAQ-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>Three bridges around the United States have been named in Edison's honor: the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Bridge_(New_Jersey)" title="Edison Bridge (New Jersey)">Edison Bridge in New Jersey</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Bridge_(Florida)" title="Edison Bridge (Florida)">Edison Bridge in Florida</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Bridge_(Ohio)" title="Edison Bridge (Ohio)">Edison Bridge in Ohio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>In space, his name is commemorated in <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> <a href="/wiki/742_Edisona" title="742 Edisona">742 Edisona</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mount_Edison" title="Mount Edison">Mount Edison</a> in the Chugach Mountains of Alaska was named after him in 1955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gnis_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-gnis-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Museums_and_memorials">Museums and memorials</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Edison_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="695" decoding="async" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Thomas_Edison_1.jpg/220px-Thomas_Edison_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Thomas_Edison_1.jpg/330px-Thomas_Edison_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Thomas_Edison_1.jpg/440px-Thomas_Edison_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Edison_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of young Thomas Edison by the railroad tracks in Port Huron, Michigan. The <a href="/wiki/Blue_Water_Bridge" title="Blue Water Bridge">Blue Water Bridge</a> can be seen in the background.</div></div></div>
<p>In West Orange, New Jersey, the 13.5 acres (5.5 hectares) Glenmont estate is maintained and operated by the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a> as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edison_National_Historic_Site" title="Edison National Historic Site">Edison National Historic Site</a>, as is his nearby laboratory and workshops including the reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Edison%27s_Black_Maria" title="Edison's Black Maria">"Black Maria"</a>—the world's first movie studio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Alva_Edison_Memorial_Tower_and_Museum" title="Thomas Alva Edison Memorial Tower and Museum">Thomas Alva Edison Memorial Tower and Museum</a> is in the town of Edison, New Jersey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Beaumont,_Texas" title="Beaumont, Texas">Beaumont, Texas</a>, there is an <a href="/wiki/Edison_Museum" title="Edison Museum">Edison Museum</a>, though Edison never visited there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Port_Huron_Museum" title="Port Huron Museum">Port Huron Museum</a>, in Port Huron, Michigan, restored the original depot that Thomas Edison worked out of as a young news butcher. The depot has been named the <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison_Depot_Museum" title="Thomas Edison Depot Museum">Thomas Edison Depot Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> The town has many Edison historical landmarks, including the graves of Edison's parents, and a monument along the <a href="/wiki/St._Clair_River" title="St. Clair River">St. Clair River</a>.
</p><p>In Detroit, the Edison Memorial Fountain in <a href="/wiki/Grand_Circus_Park_Historic_District" title="Grand Circus Park Historic District">Grand Circus Park</a> was created to honor his achievements. The limestone fountain was dedicated October 21, 1929, the fiftieth anniversary of the creation of the light bulb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> On the same night, <a href="/wiki/The_Henry_Ford" title="The Henry Ford">The Edison Institute</a> was dedicated in nearby <a href="/wiki/Dearborn,_Michigan" title="Dearborn, Michigan">Dearborn</a>.
</p><p>He was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_Hall_of_Fame" title="Automotive Hall of Fame">Automotive Hall of Fame</a> in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Thomas_Edison" title="Statue of Thomas Edison">bronze statue of Edison</a> was placed in the <a href="/wiki/National_Statuary_Hall_Collection" title="National Statuary Hall Collection">National Statuary Hall Collection</a> at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a> in 2016, with the formal dedication ceremony held on September 20 of that year. The Edison statue replaced one of 19th-century state governor <a href="/wiki/William_Allen_(governor)" title="William Allen (governor)">William Allen</a> that had been one of Ohio's two allowed contributions to the collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Companies_bearing_Edison.27s_name"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Companies_bearing_Edison's_name">Companies bearing Edison's name</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%AD%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BD_%D0%A2%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%81_%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%BE_%D0%96%D0%97%D0%9B.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2194" decoding="async" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/%D0%AD%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BD_%D0%A2%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%81_%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%BE_%D0%96%D0%97%D0%9B.JPG/200px-%D0%AD%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BD_%D0%A2%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%81_%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%BE_%D0%96%D0%97%D0%9B.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/%D0%AD%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BD_%D0%A2%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%81_%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%BE_%D0%96%D0%97%D0%9B.JPG/300px-%D0%AD%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BD_%D0%A2%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%81_%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%BE_%D0%96%D0%97%D0%9B.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/%D0%AD%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BD_%D0%A2%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%81_%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%BE_%D0%96%D0%97%D0%9B.JPG/400px-%D0%AD%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BD_%D0%A2%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%81_%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%BE_%D0%96%D0%97%D0%9B.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%AD%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BD_%D0%A2%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%81_%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%BE_%D0%96%D0%97%D0%9B.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edison in 1915</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Edison General Electric, merged with <a href="/wiki/Thomson-Houston_Electric_Company" title="Thomson-Houston Electric Company">Thomson-Houston Electric Company</a> to form <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Edison" title="Commonwealth Edison">Commonwealth Edison</a>, now part of <a href="/wiki/Exelon" title="Exelon">Exelon</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Consolidated_Edison" title="Consolidated Edison">Consolidated Edison</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Edison_International" title="Edison International">Edison International</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detroit_Edison" title="Detroit Edison">Detroit Edison</a>, a unit of <a href="/wiki/DTE_Energy" title="DTE Energy">DTE Energy</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edison_S.p.A." title="Edison S.p.A.">Edison S.p.A.</a>, a unit of Italenergia</li>
<li>Trade association the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Electric_Institute" title="Edison Electric Institute">Edison Electric Institute</a>, a lobbying and research group for investor-owned utilities in the United States</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Edison_Ore-Milling_Company" title="Edison Ore-Milling Company">Edison Ore-Milling Company</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Edison_Portland_Cement_Company" title="Edison Portland Cement Company">Edison Portland Cement Company</a></li>
<li>Ohio Edison (merged with Centerior in 1997 to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Energy" title="First Energy">First Energy</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Southern_California_Edison" title="Southern California Edison">Southern California Edison</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_named_in_honor_of_Edison">Awards named in honor of Edison</span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edison_Medal" title="Edison Medal">Edison Medal</a> was created on February 11, 1904, by a group of Edison's friends and associates. Four years later the <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Electrical_Engineers" title="American Institute of Electrical Engineers">American Institute of Electrical Engineers</a> (AIEE), later <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Electrical_and_Electronics_Engineers" title="Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers">IEEE</a>, entered into an agreement with the group to present the medal as its highest award. The first medal was presented in 1909 to <a href="/wiki/Elihu_Thomson" title="Elihu Thomson">Elihu Thomson</a>. It is the oldest award in the area of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">electrical and electronics engineering</a>, and is presented annually "for a career of meritorious achievement in electrical science, electrical engineering or the electrical arts".
</p><p>In the Netherlands, the major music awards are named the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Award" title="Edison Award">Edison Award</a> after him. The award is an annual Dutch music prize, awarded for outstanding achievements in the music industry, and is one of the oldest music awards in the world, having been presented since 1960.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Mechanical_Engineers" title="American Society of Mechanical Engineers">American Society of Mechanical Engineers</a> has awarded the Thomas A. Edison Patent Award since 1997 to individual patents that demonstrate a significant impact on the practice of mechanical engineering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_items_named_after_Edison">Other items named after Edison</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> named the <a href="/wiki/USS_Edison_(DD-439)" title="USS Edison (DD-439)">USS <i>Edison</i> (DD-439)</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gleaves_class_destroyer" title="Gleaves class destroyer">Gleaves class destroyer</a>, in his honor in 1940. The ship was decommissioned a few months after the end of World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> In 1962, the Navy commissioned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Thomas_A._Edison_(SSBN-610)" title="USS Thomas A. Edison (SSBN-610)">USS <i>Thomas A. Edison</i> (SSBN-610)</a>, a fleet ballistic missile nuclear-powered submarine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison_in_popular_culture" title="Thomas Edison in popular culture">Thomas Edison in popular culture</a></div>
<p>Thomas Edison has appeared in popular culture as a character in novels, films, comics and video games. His prolific inventing helped make him an icon and he has made appearances in popular culture during his lifetime down to the present day. Edison is also portrayed in popular culture as an adversary of <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">Nikola Tesla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 11, 2011, on what would have been Thomas Edison's 164th birthday, Google's homepage featured an animated <a href="/wiki/Google_Doodle" title="Google Doodle">Google Doodle</a> commemorating his many inventions. When the cursor was hovered over the doodle, a series of mechanisms seemed to move, causing a light bulb to glow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="People_who_worked_for_Edison">People who worked for Edison</span></h2>
<p>The following is a list of people who worked for Thomas Edison in his laboratories at Menlo Park or West Orange or at the subsidiary electrical businesses that he supervised.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Edward_Goodrich_Acheson" title="Edward Goodrich Acheson">Edward Goodrich Acheson</a> – chemist, worked at Menlo Park 1880–1884</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_Symes_Andrews" title="William Symes Andrews">William Symes Andrews</a> – started at the Menlo Park machine shop 1879</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Charles_Batchelor" title="Charles Batchelor">Charles Batchelor</a> – "chief experimental assistant"</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_I._Beggs" title="John I. Beggs">John I. Beggs</a> – manager of <a href="/wiki/Edison_Illuminating_Company" title="Edison Illuminating Company">Edison Illuminating Company</a> in New York, 1886</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_Kennedy_Dickson" title="William Kennedy Dickson">William Kennedy Dickson</a> – joined Menlo Park in 1883, worked on the motion picture camera</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Justus_B._Entz" title="Justus B. Entz">Justus B. Entz</a> – joined <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">Edison Machine Works</a> in 1887</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Reginald_Fessenden" title="Reginald Fessenden">Reginald Fessenden</a> – worked at the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">Edison Machine Works</a> in 1886</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a> – engineer <a href="/wiki/Edison_Illuminating_Company" title="Edison Illuminating Company">Edison Illuminating Company</a> Detroit, Michigan, 1891–1899</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_Joseph_Hammer" title="William Joseph Hammer">William Joseph Hammer</a> – started as laboratory assistant Menlo Park in 1879</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Miller_Reese_Hutchison" title="Miller Reese Hutchison">Miller Reese Hutchison</a> – inventor of hearing aid</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Edward_Hibberd_Johnson" title="Edward Hibberd Johnson">Edward Hibberd Johnson</a> – started in 1909, chief engineer at West Orange laboratory 1912–1918</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Samuel_Insull" title="Samuel Insull">Samuel Insull</a> – started in 1881, rose to become VP of General Electric (1892) then President of Chicago Edison</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kunihiko_Iwadare" title="Kunihiko Iwadare">Kunihiko Iwadare</a> – joined <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">Edison Machine Works</a> in 1887</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Francis_Jehl" title="Francis Jehl">Francis Jehl</a> – laboratory assistant Menlo Park 1879–1882</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arthur_E._Kennelly" title="Arthur E. Kennelly">Arthur E. Kennelly</a> – engineer, experimentalist at West Orange laboratory 1887–1894</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Kruesi" title="John Kruesi">John Kruesi</a> – started 1872, was head machinist, at Newark, Menlo Park, <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">Edison Machine Works</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lewis_Howard_Latimer" title="Lewis Howard Latimer">Lewis Howard Latimer</a> – hired 1884 as a draftsman, continued working for General Electric</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_W._Lieb" title="John W. Lieb">John W. Lieb</a> – worked at the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">Edison Machine Works</a> in 1881</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Commerford_Martin" title="Thomas Commerford Martin">Thomas Commerford Martin</a> – electrical engineer, worked at Menlo Park 1877–1879</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_F._Morrison" title="George F. Morrison">George F. Morrison</a> – started at Edison Lamp Works 1882</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwin_Stanton_Porter" title="Edwin Stanton Porter">Edwin Stanton Porter</a> – joined the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Manufacturing_Company" title="Edison Manufacturing Company">Edison Manufacturing Company</a> 1899</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frank_J._Sprague" title="Frank J. Sprague">Frank J. Sprague</a> – joined Menlo Park 1883, became known as the "Father of Electric Traction".</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">Nikola Tesla</a> – electrical engineer and inventor, worked at the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">Edison Machine Works</a> in 1884</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Francis_Robbins_Upton" title="Francis Robbins Upton">Francis Robbins Upton</a> – mathematician/physicist, joined Menlo Park 1878</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theo_Wangemann" title="Theo Wangemann">Theo Wangemann</a> – personal assistant to Edison</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Edison_Pioneers" title="Edison Pioneers">Edison Pioneers</a> – a group formed in 1918 by employees and other associates of Thomas Edison</li>

<li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1 cs1-prop-jul-greg-uncertainty">"<span class="cs1-kern-left"></span>"No Immortality of the Soul" says Thomas A. Edison. In Fact, He Doesn't Believe There Is a Soul—Human Beings Only an Aggregate of Cells and the Brain Only a Wonderful Machine, Says Wizard of Electricity". <i>The New York Times</i>. October 2, 1910. <q>Thomas A. Edison in the following interview for the first time speaks to the public on the vital subjects of the human soul and immortality. It will be bound to be a most fascinating, an amazing statement, from one of the most notable and interesting men of the age ... Nature is what we know. We do not know the gods of religions. And nature is not kind, or merciful, or loving. If God made me—the fabled God of the three qualities of which I spoke: mercy, kindness, love—He also made the fish I catch and eat. And where do His mercy, kindness, and love for that fish come in? No; nature made us—nature did it all—not the gods of the religions.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+New+York+Times&amp;rft.atitle=%22No+Immortality+of+the+Soul%22+says+Thomas+A.+Edison.+In+Fact%2C+He+Doesn%27t+Believe+There+Is+a+Soul%E2%80%94Human+Beings+Only+an+Aggregate+of+Cells+and+the+Brain+Only+a+Wonderful+Machine%2C+Says+Wizard+of+Electricity&amp;rft.date=1910-10-02&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AThomas+Edison"></span></span>
</li>

Munn &amp; Company. July 13, 1878. p. 21. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210709185502/https://books.google.com/books?id=p4o9AQAAIAAJ" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on July 9, 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 7,</span> 2021</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Scientific+American&amp;rft.pages=21&amp;rft.pub=Munn+%26+Company&amp;rft.date=1878-07-13&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dp4o9AQAAIAAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AThomas+Edison"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The same decree awarded German physicist <a href="/wiki/Hermann_von_Helmholtz" title="Hermann von Helmholtz">Hermann von Helmholtz</a> with the designation of Grand Officer of the Legion of Honor, as well as <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">Alexander Graham Bell</a>. The decree preamble cited <i>"for services provided to the Congress and to the International Electrical Exhibition"</i></span>
</li>
